Introduction - GSS Murthy

Setubandham (also called R─Бvaс╣Зavadhaс╕е) is an epic composed in Mah─Бr─Бс╣гс╣нr─л, one of the Pr─Бkс╣Ыt languages, by King Pravarasena. This epic whose subject matter is the story of Yuddhak─Бс╣Зс╕Нa of R─Бm─Бyaс╣Зa has 15 cantos called ─Б┼Ыv─Бsas with a total of 1291 verses.

The first eight cantos of the epic having 600 verses in total is being presented in this publication. The text of each verse in Mah─Бr─Бс╣гс╣нr─л and its Sanskrit ch─Бy─Б in devan─Бgar─л script are followed by its Engliish translation in prose.

The epic finds a mention in K─Бvy─Бdar┼Ыa of Daс╣Зс╕Нin (6th century AD), in Harс╣гacarita of B─Бс╣Зa (7th century AD) and in Aucityavic─Бracarc─Б of Kс╣гemendra (11th century AD), making it evident that Pravarasena lived earlier to these three renowned authors. There appear to be four kings by the name Pravarasena, the earliest of whom lived in the first century AD and the second one lived in the second century AD. Two of the four find a mention in R─Бjataraс╣Еgiс╣З─л of Kalhaс╣Зa. It is not clear who among the four was the author of this epic. However scholars agree that his time could not be later than the 5th century AD.

That Pravarasena had become famous through this work can be gauged by the fact that an inscription of the Cambodian king Ya┼Ыovarman who reigned around the end of the 9th century AD contains a verse as follows:

рдпреЗрди рдкреНрд░рд╡рд░рд╕реЗрдиреЗрди рдзрд░реНрдорд╕реЗрддреБрдВ рд╡рд┐рд╡реГрдгреНрд╡рддрд╛ рдкрд░рдГ рдкреНрд░рд╡рд░рд╕реЗрдиреЛрд╜рдкрд┐ рдЬрд┐рддрдГ рдкреНрд░рд╛рдХреГрддрд╕реЗрддреБрдХреГрддреН ред
тАЬ (The king Yashovarman) who had a distinguished army and who built a bridge of dharma excelled Pravarasena who composed Setu- k─Бvya in Pr─Бkс╣Ыt (built a natural bridge)тАЭ

The work was held in high esteem by poets and critics who came after Pravarasena. K─Бvy─Бdar┼Ыa of Daс╣Зс╕Нin contains the following verse:

рдорд╣рд╛рд░рд╛рд╖реНрдЯреНрд░рд╛рд╢реНрд░рдпрд╛рдВ рднрд╛рд╖рд╛рдВ рдкреНрд░рдХреГрд╖реНрдЯрдВ рдкреНрд░рд╛рдХреГрддрдВ рд╡рд┐рджреБрдГ ред
рд╕рд╛рдЧрд░рдГ рд╕реВрдХреНрддрд┐рд░рддреНрдирд╛рдирд╛рдВ рд╕реЗрддреБрдмрдиреНрдзрд╛рджрд┐ рдпрдиреНрдордпрдореН рее

тАЬMah─Бr─Бс╣гс╣нr─л is considered as superior Pr─Бkс╣Ыt, which is an ocean of good sayings and which contains Setubandham and other works.тАЭ Daс╣Зс╕Нin again refers to Setubandham obliquely in a passage in Avantisundar─лkath─Б to this effect:

тАЬThe citizens of kusumapura in Magadha were тАЬрди рдЬрд▓рд╛рд╢рдпрд╛рдГ рд╕реЗрддреБрдмрдиреНрдзрд▓рдЧреНрдирд╛рд╢реНрдЪтАЭ тАЬthey are not water reservoirs yet bound by the damтАЭ ( they are not dull, they study Setubandham k─БvyaтАЭ.)

In his Harс╣гacarita, B─Бс╣Зabhaс╣нс╣нa refers to the fame of Pravarasena crossing the seas:

рдХреАрд░реНрддрд┐рдГ рдкреНрд░рд╡рд░рд╕реЗрдирд╕реНрдп рдкреНрд░рдпрд╛рддрд╛ рдХреБрдореБрджреЛрдЬреНрдЬреНрд╡рд▓рд╛ ред
рд╕рд╛рдЧрд░рд╕реНрдп рдкрд░рдВ рдкрд╛рд░рдВ рдХрдкрд┐рд╕реЗрдиреЗрд╡ рд╕реЗрддреБрдирд╛ рее

тАЬSetuk─Бvya enabled the fame of Pravarasena to travel to the distant lands across the seas in the manner the construction of the bridge enabled the army of monkeys to travel to the distant land.тАЭ

PravarasenaтАЩs verses are quoted as examples by several literary critics such as Kс╣гemendra, Bhoja and others. In fact, BhojaтАЩs Sarasvat─лkaс╣Зс╣нh─Бbharaс╣Зa which is a work on poetics contains 37 different verses from Setubandham.

R─Бmad─Бsabh┼лpati who lived in the 16th century under the patronage of the Mughal emperor Akbar has written a commentary тАЬR─Бmasetuprad─лpaтАЭ in Sanskrit on this epic. It is a very lucid commentary written in a simple style. Setubandham with this commentary of R─Бmad─Бsabh┼лpati was edited by Mah─Бmahop─Бdhy─Бya ┼Ъivadatta┼Ыarma and K─Б┼Ы─лn─Бtha┼Ыarma and published by Nirс╣Зayas─Бgar press (k─Бvyam─Бl─Б-47), Mumbai in 1895. The same has been reprinted by Bh─Бrat─лya Vidy─Б Prak─Б┼Ыan, V─Бr─Бс╣Зas─л later. Earlier to that, Prof. S. Goldschmit had critically edited and published the original Pr─Бkс╣Ыt text in 1880 at Strassbourg, France (then in Germany). It did not contain the Sanskrit ch─Бy─Б or the commentary of R─Бmad─Бsabh┼лpati. He published a second volume which contained the German translation of the work in 1884. In 1976 Prof. Krishna Kanta Handiqui translated Setubandham into English and published it with extracts from unpublished commentaries, critical notes and scholarly and exhaustive introduction (Pr─Бkс╣Ыt Text Society, Ahmedabad, Pr─Бkс╣Ыt Text Series-20). This, however, did not contain the Pr─Бkс╣Ыt text, Sanskrit ch─Бy─Б or the Sanskrit commentary. Prof.Handiqui refers to commentaries on Setubandham written by Kс╣Ыс╣гс╣Зavipra, Mudamallabhaс╣нс╣нa, M─Бdhavayajvan and others. He also discusses three recensions of the work namely the southern, eastern and western, the western recension being the basis for the commentary of R─Бmad─Бsabh┼лpati.

My English rendering in prose of the first eight cantos (600 verses) is based on the printed book brought out by Bh─Бrat─лya Vidy─Б Prak─Б┼Ыan, V─Бr─Бс╣Зas─л. As I have no knowledge of Mah─Бr─Бс╣гс╣нr─л, in which the original is written, my rendering is totally dependant upon the Sanskrit commentary of R─Бmad─Бsabh┼лpati. I have tried to make my rendering as close to the Sanskrit ch─Бy─Б as possible subject to the constraints of English. My translation is not influenced either by GoldschmitтАЩs edition or by HandiquiтАЩs edition , as I was not aware of the existence of these earlier editions when I did the translation. However in this introduction I have drawn material from the scholarly introduction given by Prof.Handiqui in his work.

Although the first eight cantos of the epic cover the story of R─Бm─Бyaс╣Зa from R─БmaтАЩs pining for S─лt─Б during the rainy season to NalaтАЩs construction of the bridge across the ocean, the poetтАЩs narration of the story, although quite sketchy, differs from that of V─Бlm─лki. It raises a doubt in the mind of the reader if Pravarasena followed a Ramayana other than that of V─Бlm─лki. What distinguishes this epic is the manner the poet utilizes the story to unleash his imagination through detailed descriptions such as that of autumn (Canto-1), the ocean (Canto-2), Sugr─лvaтАЩs exhortation to the monkeys (Canto-3), reactions of monkey-chiefs to Sugr─лvaтАЩs address and Vibh─лс╣гaс╣ЗaтАЩs arrival (Canto-4), the cataclysmic effects of R─БmaтАЩs shooting of arrows at the ocean (Canto-5), the appearance of the Ocean-god who suggests that a bridge may be built and the activities of the monkeys in lifting mountains and bringing them to the ocean (Canto-6), their unsuccessful attempt to build the bridge (Canto-7) and their ultimate success in constructing the bridge under the direction of Nala, commanded by Sugr─лva (Canto-8).

Like in many other epics in Sanskrit, the story becomes just a frame-work to support decorations and embellishments in the form of descriptions of seasons, nature and battles centred around sentiments of love and valour. The reader may often feel bewildered at the weird and contrived imageries offered by the poet. However the ease with which the poet successfully conveys through elegant poetry his complex imagery is truly astounding. The poetтАЩs diction as reflected in the Sanskrit ch─Бy─Б and his effortless stringing of long compound words of mostly soft syllables are very pleasing. The poet has composed the work mostly in the Metre skambhaka, an ─Бry─Б-like Metre where each quarter of a verse consists of 4-m─Бtr─Б gaс╣Зas, the odd quarters containing 3 units and even quarters containing 5 units and this choice has enhanced the rhythmic flow of the verses. An interesting feature of this epic is that the last verse of every canto contains the word, тАЬanur─БgaтАЭ( ch─Бy─Б).

It is necessary to mention here a discrepancy that has crept in this commentary (as brought out in the printed edition) regarding the authorship of this epic. The first two cantos end as follows: тАЬрдЗрддрд┐ рд╢реНрд░реАрдкреНрд░рд╡рд░рд╕реЗрдирд╡рд┐рд░рдЪрд┐рддреЗ рджрд╢рдореБрдЦрд╡рдзреЗ рдорд╣рд╛рдХрд╛рд╡реНрдпреЗ рдкреНрд░рдердо рдЖрд╢реНрд╡рд╛рд╕рдХрдГ рд╕рдорд╛рдкреНрддрдГтАЭ and тАЬрдЗрддрд┐ рд╢реНрд░реАрдкреНрд░рд╡рд░рд╕реЗрдирд╡рд┐рд░рдЪрд┐рддреЗ рджрд╢рдореБрдЦрд╡рдзреЗ рдорд╣рд╛рдХрд╛рд╡реНрдпреЗ рджреНрд╡рд┐рддреАрдп рдЖрд╢реНрд╡рд╛рд╕рдХрдГ рд╕рдорд╛рдкреНрддрдГтАЭ. But from the third canto onwards the ending is different with the addition of рдХрд╛рд▓рд┐рджрд╛рд╕рдХреГрддреЗ and reads as follows тАЬрдЗрддрд┐ рд╢реНрд░реАрдкреНрд░рд╡рд░рд╕реЗрдирд╡рд┐рд░рдЪрд┐рддреЗ рдХрд╛рд▓рд┐рджрд╛рд╕рдХреГрддреЗ рджрд╢рдореБрдЦрд╡рдзреЗ рдорд╣рд╛рдХрд╛рд╡реНрдпреЗ —- рд╕рдорд╛рдкреНрддрдГтАЭ. Is it meant to convey that Pravarasena started the work and K─Бlid─Бsa completed it? One of the introductory verses of the commentator conveys that as desired by king Vikram─Бditya, K─Бlid─Бsa composed this work ! Right at the time of the commentator, the epic had been attributed to K─Бlid─Бsa ! However scholars are in agreement that when looked at from the point of style or generally accepted chronology of Sanskrit poets, this work could not have been composed by K─Бlid─Бsa.

Having had an adversary (in R─Бma), when the Goddess of royal wealth residing in the exceedingly proud heart of V─Бl─л was hit by the arrow of Love God in the form of R─Бma, she departed to reside in Sugr─лva. R─Бma, son of Da┼Ыaratha somehow (with difficulty) spent the rainy days which happened to be night for the Sun called тАШactivityтАЩ, which was a strong binding chain for the elephant called тАШanger of R─БmaтАЩ and which was a cage for the lion called тАШvictory (of R─Бma)тАЩ. [The poet imagines that R─БmaтАЩs arrow that hit V─Бl─л became an arrow of love that affected the Goddess of royal prosperity and made her fall in love with Sugr─лva. If the monkeys do not cross the ocean they along with R─Бma will all have to stay put there as they cannot go back thus suffering the unpleasantness of MoonтАЩs rays.]

While your enthusiasm for war gets delayed, my royal lustre does not even now fructify and is still a bud like a recently grown creeper which does not bear fruit when the season gets delayed. English (GSS Murthy)

The shadow of sadness does not stay for long in a virtuous person, whose fame shines all round and whose valour extends to the whole world just as darkness (due to eclipse) does not stay for long in the Sun who spreads light all around and whose heat extends to the whole world of living beings.

The demons who roam around during nights are not fit to see the army of monkeys just as the particles of smoke are not fit to see the morning Sun, which has the colour of red sparks shaken out of a cinder.

What to say can we say of discussions on activity, which are suffering from deviations from standards and which cannot be set right and which lead to dissipation although they may be rich in content like oceans? [Just as oceans dissipate their energy by overflowing without leading to any constructive result, ill-planned activity comes to naught.]

English (GSS Murthy)

My knowledge which is pure due to being based on scriptures and which is stable even if disturbed and which may be unseen is weightier than your experience which may be evident and which may be placed at the tip of the balance.

English (GSS Murthy)

Whatever equal strength and valour can achieve together cannot be achieved if they are disorganized. One Sun can provide sunshine strongly but twelve Suns together can destroy all the three worlds. [Reference is to all the twelve Adityas acting together to destroy the universe at the time of the great annihilation.]

English (GSS Murthy)

Enthusiasm applied under inappropriate circumstances goes against one’s pride and also does not create fear in the enemy like an arrow hastily released in anger.

O brave Sugr─лva! By being hasty, you would be going against the proper conduct of a royal. Even the SunтАЩs prowess (heat) gets reduced when he moves across the sky hastily during his southern sojourn (winter months).

English (GSS Murthy)

Goddess of Victory may get disinterested in you if you display your vanity through stories of unmanly ways of fighting just like a woman, who happily comes to her man with her head bent would get disinterested when she hears her rivalтАЩs name mentioned by her man due to a slip. (Do not indulge in vain gossip of glory without appropriate action.)

English (GSS Murthy)

Do not indulge solely in haste. The fame of Moon arising solely out of its beneficial effect on blue lotuses gets diluted because of its adverse effect on red lotuses.

English (GSS Murthy)

Should one personally attack the followers of the opponent or should oneтАЩs followers attack the opponent? Which is appropriate? In the former option, of what value is victory? ( It is better for Sugr─лva not to directly engage in a battle with the enemy.)

English (GSS Murthy)

O brave one! Do you, being the leader of chief monkeys like Hanum─Бn, have to accomplish something that exceeds what Hanum─Бn accomplished with his fame unblemished? (You do not have to.)

рдХрд╣ рддрдореНрдорд┐ рд╡рд┐ рд▓рд╛рдЗрдЬреНрдЬтАНрдЗ рдЬрдореНрдорд┐ рдЕтАНрдЗрдгреНрдгрдкреНрдлрд▓рд╛ рдЕрджреВрд░рдкрд╕рд░рд┐рдЖ ред

How can an order be given to a person by whom a job entrusted (earlier) did not yield far reaching results? A creeper seeks another tree again if the tree which it had entwined falls down.

English (GSS Murthy)

O Chief of monkeys! In your desire to do something to please R─Бma why do you want to do the opposite by wanting to kill R─Бvaс╣Зa the ten-headed while R─Бma himself is eager to kill him?

English (GSS Murthy)

Having thus restrained Sugr─лva, J─Бmbav─Бn son of Brahma moved away from R─БmaтАЩs side and faced him in the manner the swarm of smoke at the time of pralaya faces the Sun after splashing the peak of Meru Mountain.
